Abstract Extraction

Steps:

1. Find the Most Important Words

2. Compute a significance score for sentences based on words they contain

3. Pick the topmost significant sentences

Word Importance

Authors tend to repeat the words that are important to the theme of the text.

Word Importance = Word Frequency

Sentence Significance

Sentences which encapsulate more of the important words are more significant.

Significance Score = Sum(Word Importance)

Pick the top most significant sentences.

fill-rule="evenodd"></path></svg></span></a></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="mb-md gray-dark font--subhead font-xxs"><div class="display-date">October 13, 2018 at 12:45 PM EDT</div></div><div class="article-body"><div class="teaser-content"><section><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">KABUL — The Trump administration’s special adviser on Afghan peace met last week with Taliban representatives in Qatar for talks that included “working toward finding a peaceful resolution” to the war, a spokesman for the insurgent group said Saturday.</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The spokesman, Zabiullah Mujahid, said the presence of foreign troops in Afghanistan remains the “greatest obstacle” to peace, but he also noted that both sides agreed to continue the dialogue — a potentially major step toward formal negotiations with the Taliban to end the 17-year war.</p></section></div><div class="remainder-content"><section><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The U.S. Embassy in Kabul said in a statement that U.S. envoy Zalmay Khalilzad held “consultations” last week in Islamabad, Pakistan; Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; and Doha, Qatar. The embassy did not confirm the reported meeting with Taliban officials.</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">It said Khalilzad returned to Kabul for follow-up meetings with Afghan President Ashraf Ghani and other officials, as well as political and civic groups, “to hear their views and priorities on a settlement.” Ghani’s office confirmed that scenario.</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb"></wp-ad></div></div><div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The reported talks would mark another significant outreach to Taliban representatives in Qatar, which has sought to act as a mediator. A first breakthrough meeting was held there in July with Alice Wells, a senior State Department official. U.S. officials have never publicly confirmed the meeting.</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Khalilzad’s reported meeting came just weeks after he was named Washington’s point man on Afghan peace. He first visited Kabul and Islamabad, the Pakistani capital, early last week, then vanished from public view. His published schedule also included visits to Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates, but U.S. officials have not commented on those. </p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_2"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md interstitial italic"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/asia_pacific/in-western-afghanistan-villagers-are-fleeing-not-just-war-but-drought/2018/09/24/0caa6b8a-bc39-11e8-8243-f3ae9c99658a_story.html?utm_term=.e43d1c64207c&amp;tid=lk_interstitial_manual_10">In western Afghanistan, villagers flee both war and drought</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">The potentially encouraging news from Doha came as the latest violent attack targeted Afghan candidates for parliamentary elections scheduled for Saturday. A bombing at a campaign rally in northern Takhar province killed more than 20 people. No group claimed it, but the Taliban warned that it would seek to disrupt the polls, and more than 300 people have already died in election-related attacks.</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_2"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Khalilzad’s public one-day visit Monday to Pakistan resulted in little more than platitudes. Officials there were polite but cool, and Pakistan’s foreign minister stated in Washington that Khalilzad — who once suggested the country be declared a terrorist state — needs to be more “sensitive” in his new role.</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Pakistan has long said it seeks peace in Afghanistan, but Afghan and U.S. officials have accused it of covertly sheltering anti-Afghan militants, and Washington has suspended $300 million in military aid. A key part of Khalilzad’s assignment is to persuade Pakistan to actively back peace talks. </p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_3"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">In Afghanistan — where Khalilzad was born and served as U.S. ambassador in the early 2000s — his previous diplomatic tenure was marred by accusations of political strong-arming. But last week, his pro-peace agenda earned a cordial reception. Ghani has long sought to revive talks with the Taliban, and hopes for progress soared after a first-ever three-day truce in June.</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_3"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Khalilzad told journalists in Kabul on Monday that Afghan and Taliban leaders should name their negotiating teams. He said the United States would be happy to participate in any talks, but that the peace process should remain “Afghan-owned.”</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">His message stood in sharp contrast to that of <a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/national-security/erik-prince-in-kabul-pushes-privatization-of-the-afghan-war/2018/10/04/72a76d36-c7e5-11e8-b1ed-1d2d65b86d0c_story.html?utm_term=.cea514cf257a&amp;tid=lk_inline_manual_19" target="_self">Erik Prince</a>, the American security contractor who visited Kabul two weeks ago to promote privatizing the armed conflict. In an interview on Afghan TV, the former Blackwater chief boasted that his fighters could turn around the war in six months. Afghan reaction was uniformly negative, and U.S. military officials have dismissed the idea. </p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_4"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“In some respects, Khalilzad is the perfect person for this job. He understands the terrain, the politics, and the state of play in Pakistan and the region,” said Michael Kugelman, an expert on Pakistan and Afghanistan at the Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars in Washington. </p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_4"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md interstitial italic"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/asia_pacific/weeks-away-critical-afghan-elections-threatened-by-violence-claims-of-manipulation/2018/09/04/cf6cbb4e-ad59-11e8-b1da-ff7faa680710_story.html?utm_term=.a6875ff579a0&amp;tid=lk_interstitial_manual_23">Violence, claims of ma­nipu­la­tion ahead of Afghan elections</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Kugelman noted, though, that his past criticisms of Pakistan could hinder Khalilzad’s ability to “make headway with Islamabad.” Other analysts said gaining the support of other regional powers is also needed to settle the conflict and that it will require more than one-man shuttle diplomacy.</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">“Mr. Khalilzad cannot do a lot,” said Ahmad Zia Rafat, a university professor in Kabul. “The question is, are the main players — such as the U.S., Iran, Pakistan, India, China and Iran — ready for peace here or not?”</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns db dn-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_mob_bigbox_5"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined">Kugelman emphasized that within Afghanistan, too, the insurgents’ continued attacks and widening control of territory remain stubborn obstacles to Khalilzad’s mission.</p><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md undefined"><span>“We shouldn’t overstate his ability to move mountains,” he said. “The fundamental question remains the same: how to convince the Taliban to stop fighting when it</span> thinks it’s winning the war.”</p><div class="cb bg-offwhite mt-xxs pad-top-md pad-bottom-md mb-lg ml-neg-gutter mr-neg-gutter mr-auto-ns ml-auto-ns dn db-ns relative" style="min-height:250px"><div class="absolute right-0 bottom-0 gray-dark pad-right-xs pad-bottom-xs">AD</div><div class=" "><wp-ad class="hide-for-print hide-for-print" id="slug_inline_bb_5"></wp-ad></div></div><p class="font--body font-copy color-gray-darkest ma-0 pad-bottom-md italic">Sayed Salahuddin in Kabul and Shaiq Hussain in Islamabad contributed to this report.</p><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/asia_pacific/recruited-by-iran-to-fight-for-syrian-regime-young-afghans-bring-home-cash-and-scars/2018/07/29/ecf9e34c-64e0-11e8-81ca-bb14593acaa6_story.html?utm_term=.e1a5b261c001&amp;tid=lk_interstitial_manual_32">Young Afghan fighters return from Syria with cash and scars</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md"><a href="https://www.washingtonpost.com/world/us-military-command-changes-hands-in-afghanistan/2018/09/02/3bb73b42-ae29-11e8-8a0c-70b618c98d3c_story.html?utm_term=.56982c079619&amp;tid=lk_interstitial_manual_33">U.S. military command changes hands in Afghanistan</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md"><a of State Pompeo tries to ‘reset’ relations with Pakistan</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md"><a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/world?tid=lk_interstitial_manual_35">Today’s coverage from Post correspondents around the world</a></p><p class="font--body font-copy hide-for-print ma-0 mb-md"><a Out[6]:
"The then-U.S. Ambassador to the United Nations, Zalmay Khalilzad, speaks to reporters at U.N. headquarters in 2009. (Mary Altaffer/AP)By Pamela Constable\xa0closePamela ConstableForeign correspondent covering Afghanistan, Pakistan, South Asia, Latin America and immigrationEmailEmailBioBioFollowFollowOctober 13, 2018 at 12:45 PM EDTKABUL — The Trump administration’s special adviser on Afghan peace met last week with Taliban representatives in Qatar for talks that included “working toward finding a peaceful resolution” to the war, a spokesman for the insurgent group said Saturday.The spokesman, Zabiullah Mujahid, said the\xa0presence of foreign troops in Afghanistan remains the “greatest obstacle” to peace, but he also noted that both sides agreed to continue the dialogue — a potentially major step toward formal negotiations with the Taliban to end the 17-year war.The U.S. Embassy in Kabul said in a statement that U.S. envoy Zalmay Khalilzad held “consultations” last week in Islamabad, Pakistan; Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; and Doha, Qatar. The embassy did not confirm the reported meeting with Taliban officials.ADIt said Khalilzad returned to Kabul for follow-up meetings with Afghan President Ashraf Ghani and other officials, as well as political and civic groups, “to hear their views and priorities on a settlement.” Ghani’s office confirmed that scenario.ADThe reported talks would mark another significant outreach to Taliban representatives in Qatar, which has sought to act as a mediator. A first breakthrough meeting was held there in July with Alice Wells, a senior State Department official. U.S. officials have never publicly confirmed the meeting.Khalilzad’s reported meeting came just weeks after he was named Washington’s point man on Afghan peace. He first visited Kabul and Islamabad, the Pakistani capital, early last week, then vanished from public view. His published schedule also included visits to Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates, but U.S. officials have not commented on those.\xa0ADIn western Afghanistan, villagers flee both war and droughtThe potentially encouraging news from Doha came as the latest violent attack targeted Afghan candidates for parliamentary elections scheduled for Saturday.\xa0A bombing at a campaign rally in northern Takhar province killed more than 20 people. No group claimed it, but the Taliban warned that it would seek to disrupt the polls, and more than 300 people have already died in election-related attacks.ADKhalilzad’s public one-day visit Monday to Pakistan resulted in little more than platitudes. Officials there were polite but cool, and Pakistan’s\xa0foreign minister stated in Washington that Khalilzad — who once suggested the country\xa0be declared a terrorist state — needs to be more “sensitive” in his new role.Pakistan has long said it seeks peace in Afghanistan, but Afghan and U.S. officials have accused it of covertly sheltering anti-Afghan militants, and Washington has suspended $300\xa0million in military aid. A key part of Khalilzad’s assignment is to persuade Pakistan to actively back peace talks.\xa0ADIn Afghanistan — where Khalilzad was born and served as U.S. ambassador in the early 2000s — his previous diplomatic tenure was marred by accusations of political strong-arming.\xa0But last week, his pro-peace agenda earned a cordial reception. Ghani has long sought to revive talks with the Taliban, and hopes for progress soared after a first-ever three-day truce in June.ADKhalilzad told journalists in Kabul on Monday that Afghan and Taliban leaders should name their negotiating teams. He said the United States would be happy to participate in any talks, but that the peace process should remain “Afghan-owned.”His message stood in sharp contrast to that of\xa0Erik Prince, the American security contractor who visited Kabul two weeks ago to promote privatizing the armed conflict. In an interview on Afghan TV, the former Blackwater chief boasted that his fighters could turn around the war in six months. Afghan reaction was uniformly negative, and U.S. military officials have dismissed the idea.\xa0AD“In some respects, Khalilzad is the perfect person for this job. He understands the terrain, the politics, and the state of play in Pakistan and the region,” said Michael Kugelman, an expert on Pakistan and Afghanistan at the Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars in Washington.\xa0ADViolence, claims of ma\xadnipu\xadla\xadtion ahead of Afghan electionsKugelman noted, though, that his past criticisms of Pakistan could hinder Khalilzad’s ability to “make headway with Islamabad.” Other analysts said gaining the support of other regional powers is also needed to settle the conflict and that it will require more than one-man shuttle diplomacy.“Mr. Khalilzad cannot do a lot,” said Ahmad Zia Rafat, a university professor in Kabul. “The question is, are the main players — such as the U.S., Iran, Pakistan, India, China and Iran — ready for peace here or not?”ADKugelman emphasized that within Afghanistan, too, the insurgents’ continued attacks and widening control of territory remain stubborn obstacles to Khalilzad’s mission.“We shouldn’t overstate his ability to move mountains,” he said. “The fundamental question remains the same: how to convince the Taliban to stop fighting when it thinks it’s winning the war.”ADSayed Salahuddin in Kabul and Shaiq Hussain in Islamabad contributed to this report.Young Afghan fighters return from Syria with cash and scarsU.S. military command changes hands in AfghanistanSecretary of State Pompeo tries to ‘reset’ relations with PakistanToday’s coverage from Post correspondents around the worldLike Washington Post World on Facebook and stay updated on foreign newsADADcomments34 CommentsToday's HeadlinesFree daily updates delivered just for youBy signing up you agree to our Terms of Use and Privacy\xa0PolicyhomeHomeshareShareShare on FacebookEmail this linkShare on TwitterShare on PinterestShare on LinkedIn"

'b"The then-U.S. Ambassador to the United Nations, Zalmay Khalilzad, speaks to reporters at U.N. headquarters in 2009. (Mary Altaffer/AP)By Pamela Constable closePamela ConstableForeign correspondent covering Afghanistan, Pakistan, South Asia, Latin America and immigrationEmailEmailBioBioFollowFollowOctober 13, 2018 at 12:45 PM EDTKABUL   The Trump administration s special adviser on Afghan peace met last week with Taliban representatives in Qatar for talks that included  working toward finding a peaceful resolution  to the war, a spokesman for the insurgent group said Saturday.The spokesman, Zabiullah Mujahid, said the presence of foreign troops in Afghanistan remains the  greatest obstacle  to peace, but he also noted that both sides agreed to continue the dialogue   a potentially major step toward formal negotiations with the Taliban to end the 17-year war.The U.S. Embassy in Kabul said in a statement that U.S. envoy Zalmay Khalilzad held  consultations  last week in Islamabad, Pakistan; Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; and Doha, Qatar. The embassy did not confirm the reported meeting with Taliban officials.ADIt said Khalilzad returned to Kabul for follow-up meetings with Afghan President Ashraf Ghani and other officials, as well as political and civic groups,  to hear their views and priorities on a settlement.  Ghani s office confirmed that scenario.ADThe reported talks would mark another significant outreach to Taliban representatives in Qatar, which has sought to act as a mediator. A first breakthrough meeting was held there in July with Alice Wells, a senior State Department official. U.S. officials have never publicly confirmed the meeting.Khalilzad s reported meeting came just weeks after he was named Washington s point man on Afghan peace. He first visited Kabul and Islamabad, the Pakistani capital, early last week, then vanished from public view. His published schedule also included visits to Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates, but U.S. officials have not commented on those. ADIn western Afghanistan, villagers flee both war and droughtThe potentially encouraging news from Doha came as the latest violent attack targeted Afghan candidates for parliamentary elections scheduled for Saturday. A bombing at a campaign rally in northern Takhar province killed more than 20 people. No group claimed it, but the Taliban warned that it would seek to disrupt the polls, and more than 300 people have already died in election-related attacks.ADKhalilzad s public one-day visit Monday to Pakistan resulted in little more than platitudes. Officials there were polite but cool, and Pakistan s foreign minister stated in Washington that Khalilzad   who once suggested the country be declared a terrorist state   needs to be more  sensitive  in his new role.Pakistan has long said it seeks peace in Afghanistan, but Afghan and U.S. officials have accused it of covertly sheltering anti-Afghan militants, and Washington has suspended $300 million in military aid. A key part of Khalilzad s assignment is to persuade Pakistan to actively back peace talks. ADIn Afghanistan   where Khalilzad was born and served as U.S. ambassador in the early 2000s   his previous diplomatic tenure was marred by accusations of political strong-arming. But last week, his pro-peace agenda earned a cordial reception. Ghani has long sought to revive talks with the Taliban, and hopes for progress soared after a first-ever three-day truce in June.ADKhalilzad told journalists in Kabul on Monday that Afghan and Taliban leaders should name their negotiating teams. He said the United States would be happy to participate in any talks, but that the peace process should remain  Afghan-owned. His message stood in sharp contrast to that of Erik Prince, the American security contractor who visited Kabul two weeks ago to promote privatizing the armed conflict. In an interview on Afghan TV, the former Blackwater chief boasted that his fighters could turn around the war in six months. Afghan reaction was uniformly negative, and U.S. military officials have dismissed the idea. AD In some respects, Khalilzad is the perfect person for this job. He understands the terrain, the politics, and the state of play in Pakistan and the region,  said Michael Kugelman, an expert on Pakistan and Afghanistan at the Woodrow Wilson International Center for Scholars in Washington. ADViolence, claims of ma nipu la tion ahead of Afghan electionsKugelman noted, though, that his past criticisms of Pakistan could hinder Khalilzad s ability to  make headway with Islamabad.  Other analysts said gaining the support of other regional powers is also needed to settle the conflict and that it will require more than one-man shuttle diplomacy. Mr. Khalilzad cannot do a lot,  said Ahmad Zia Rafat, a university professor in Kabul.  The question is, are the main players   such as the U.S., Iran, Pakistan, India, China and Iran   ready for peace here or not  ADKugelman emphasized that within Afghanistan, too, the insurgents  continued attacks and widening control of territory remain stubborn obstacles to Khalilzad s mission. We shouldn t overstate his ability to move mountains,  he said.  The fundamental question remains the same: how to convince the Taliban to stop fighting when it thinks it s winning the war. ADSayed Salahuddin in Kabul and Shaiq Hussain in Islamabad contributed to this report.Young Afghan fighters return from Syria with cash and scarsU.S. military command changes hands in AfghanistanSecretary of State Pompeo tries to  reset  relations with PakistanToday s coverage from Post correspondents around the worldLike Washington Post World on Facebook and stay updated on foreign newsADADcomments34 CommentsToday\'s HeadlinesFree daily updates delivered just for youBy signing up you agree to our Terms of Use and Privacy PolicyhomeHomeshareShareShare on FacebookEmail this linkShare on TwitterShare on PinterestShare on LinkedIn"'
In [9]:
def getTextWaPost(url):
    page = urllib.request.urlopen(articleURL).read().decode('utf8','ignore')
    soup = BeautifulSoup(page,'lxml')
    text = ' '.join(map(lambda p: p.text, soup.find_all('article')))
    str(text.encode('ascii',errors='replace')).replace("\xa0"," ")
    return str(text.encode('ascii',errors='replace')).replace("?"," ")
In [10]:
text = getTextWaPost(articleURL)

In [21]:
from collections import defaultdict

ranking = defaultdict(int)
for i,sent in enumerate(sents):
    for w in word_tokenize(sent.lower()):
        if w in freq:
            ranking[i] += freq[w]
ranking

['(Mary Altaffer/AP)By Pamela Constable closePamela ConstableForeign correspondent covering Afghanistan, Pakistan, South Asia, Latin America and immigrationEmailEmailBioBioFollowFollowOctober 13, 2018 at 12:45 PM EDTKABUL   The Trump administration s special adviser on Afghan peace met last week with Taliban representatives in Qatar for talks that included  working toward finding a peaceful resolution  to the war, a spokesman for the insurgent group said Saturday.The spokesman, Zabiullah Mujahid, said the presence of foreign troops in Afghanistan remains the  greatest obstacle  to peace, but he also noted that both sides agreed to continue the dialogue   a potentially major step toward formal negotiations with the Taliban to end the 17-year war.The U.S. Embassy in Kabul said in a statement that U.S. envoy Zalmay Khalilzad held  consultations  last week in Islamabad, Pakistan; Riyadh, Saudi Arabia; and Doha, Qatar.',
 'The embassy did not confirm the reported meeting with Taliban officials.ADIt said Khalilzad returned to Kabul for follow-up meetings with Afghan President Ashraf Ghani and other officials, as well as political and civic groups,  to hear their views and priorities on a settlement.',
 'Officials there were polite but cool, and Pakistan s foreign minister stated in Washington that Khalilzad   who once suggested the country be declared a terrorist state   needs to be more  sensitive  in his new role.Pakistan has long said it seeks peace in Afghanistan, but Afghan and U.S. officials have accused it of covertly sheltering anti-Afghan militants, and Washington has suspended $300 million in military aid.',
 'The question is, are the main players   such as the U.S., Iran, Pakistan, India, China and Iran   ready for peace here or not  ADKugelman emphasized that within Afghanistan, too, the insurgents  continued attacks and widening control of territory remain stubborn obstacles to Khalilzad s mission.']
